CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Polarization (waves)</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Polarization_(disambiguation)" title="Polarization (disambiguation)">Polarization</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarizacio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Polarizacio.jpg/220px-Polarizacio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Polarizacio.jpg/330px-Polarizacio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Polarizacio.jpg/440px-Polarizacio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polarizacio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Circular polarization on rubber thread, converted to linear polarization</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Polarization</b> (<a href="/wiki/American_and_British_English_spelling_differences" title="American and British English spelling differences">also</a> <b>polarisation</b>) is a property applying to <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">transverse</a> <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">waves</a> that specifies the geometrical orientation of the <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">oscillations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shipman_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shipman-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muncaster_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Muncaster-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avadhanulu_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Avadhanulu-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Desmarais_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Desmarais-5">[5]</a></sup>  In a transverse wave, the direction of the oscillation is perpendicular to the direction of motion of the wave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avadhanulu_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Avadhanulu-4">[4]</a></sup>  A simple example of a polarized transverse wave is vibrations traveling along a taut string <i>(see image)</i>; for example, in a musical instrument like a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guitar_string" title="Guitar string">guitar string</a>. Depending on how the string is plucked, the vibrations can be in a vertical direction, horizontal direction, or at any angle perpendicular to the string.  In contrast, in <a href="/wiki/Longitudinal_wave" title="Longitudinal wave">longitudinal waves</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_wave" title="Sound wave">sound waves</a> in a liquid or gas, the displacement of the particles in the oscillation is always in the direction of propagation, so these waves do not exhibit polarization.  Transverse waves that exhibit polarization include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">electromagnetic waves</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational waves</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and transverse sound waves (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shear_wave" title="Shear wave">shear waves</a>) in solids.  In some types of transverse waves, the wave displacement is limited to a single direction, so these also do not exhibit polarization; for example, in surface waves in liquids (<a href="/wiki/Gravity_wave" title="Gravity wave">gravity waves</a>), the wave displacement of the particles is always in a vertical plane.\n</p><p>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">electromagnetic wave</a> such as light consists of a coupled oscillating <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> which are always perpendicular; by convention, the "polarization" of electromagnetic waves refers to the direction of the electric field.  In <a href="/wiki/Linear_polarization" title="Linear polarization">linear polarization</a>, the fields oscillate in a single direction.  In <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">circular</a> or <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_polarization" title="Elliptical polarization">elliptical polarization</a>, the fields rotate at a constant rate in a plane as the wave travels.  The rotation can have two possible directions; if the fields rotate in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_hand_rule" title="Right hand rule">right hand</a> sense with respect to the direction of wave travel, it is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_circular_polarization" title="Right circular polarization">right circular polarization</a>, or, if the fields rotate in a left hand sense, it is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_circular_polarization" title="Left circular polarization">left circular polarization</a>.\n</p><p>Light or other electromagnetic radiation from many sources, such as the sun, flames, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incandescent_lamp" title="Incandescent lamp">incandescent lamps</a>, consists of short wave trains with an equal mixture of polarizations; this is called <i>unpolarized light</i>.  Polarized light can be produced by passing unpolarized light through a <a href="/wiki/Polarizer" title="Polarizer">polarizer</a>, which allows waves of only one polarization to pass through.   The most common optical materials (such as glass) are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a> and do not affect the polarization of light passing through them; however, some materials&#8212;those that exhibit <a href="/wiki/Birefringence" title="Birefringence">birefringence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dichroism" title="Dichroism">dichroism</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_activity" title="Optical activity">optical activity</a>&#8212;can change the polarization of light.  Some of these are used to make polarizing filters.  Light is also partially polarized when it reflects from a surface.\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, electromagnetic waves can also be viewed as streams of particles called <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>.  When viewed in this way, the polarization of an electromagnetic wave is determined by a quantum mechanical property of photons called their <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lipson_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lipson-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waldman_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waldman-8">[8]</a></sup>  A photon has one of two possible spins: it can either spin in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_hand_rule" title="Right hand rule">right hand</a> sense or a left hand sense about its direction of travel. Circularly polarized electromagnetic waves are composed of photons with only one type of spin, either right- or left-hand.  Linearly polarized waves consist of photons that are in a superposition of right and left circularly polarized states, with equal amplitude and phases synchronized to give oscillation in a plane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waldman_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Waldman-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Polarization is an important parameter in areas of science dealing with transverse waves, such as <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwaves</a>. Especially impacted are technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a>, wireless and optical fiber <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telecommunications" title="Telecommunications">telecommunications</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><span id="Theory"></span>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave_propagation_and_polarization">Wave propagation and polarization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Wave propagation and polarization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Most sources of light are classified as incoherent and unpolarized (or only "partially polarized") because they consist of a random mixture of waves having different spatial characteristics, frequencies (wavelengths), phases, and polarization states. However, for understanding electromagnetic waves and polarization in particular, it is easiest to just consider coherent <a href="/wiki/Plane_wave" title="Plane wave">plane waves</a>; these are sinusoidal waves of one particular direction (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wavevector" title="Wavevector">wavevector</a>), frequency, phase, and polarization state. Characterizing an optical system in relation to a plane wave with those given parameters can then be used to predict its response to a more general case, since a wave with any specified spatial structure can be decomposed into a combination of plane waves (its so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angular_spectrum" title="Angular spectrum">angular spectrum</a>). And incoherent states can be modeled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stochastically" title="Stochastically">stochastically</a> as a weighted combination of such uncorrelated waves with some <a href="/wiki/Probability_distribution" title="Probability distribution">distribution</a> of frequencies (its <i><a href="/wiki/Spectral_density" title="Spectral density">spectrum</a></i>), phases, and polarizations.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transverse_electromagnetic_waves">Transverse electromagnetic waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Transverse electromagnetic waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:602px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electromagnetic_wave.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Electromagnetic_wave.png/600px-Electromagnetic_wave.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/Electromagnetic_wave.png 1.5x" width="600"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electromagnetic_wave.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A "vertically polarized" electromagnetic wave of wavelength &#955; has its electric field vector <b>E</b> (red) oscillating in the vertical direction. The magnetic field <b>B</b> (or <b>H</b>) is always at right angles to it (blue), and both are perpendicular to the direction of propagation (<b>z</b>).</div></div></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_waves" title="Electromagnetic waves">Electromagnetic waves</a> (such as light), traveling in free space or another <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_(physics)" title="Homogeneity (physics)">homogeneous</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a> <a href="/wiki/Attenuation" title="Attenuation">non-attenuating</a> medium, are properly described as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transverse_waves" title="Transverse waves">transverse waves</a>, meaning that a plane wave\'s electric field vector <b>E</b> and magnetic field <b>H</b> are in directions perpendicular to (or "transverse" to) the direction of wave propagation; <b>E</b> and <b>H</b> are also perpendicular to each other.  By convention, the "polarization" direction of an electromagnetic wave is given by its electric field vector.  Considering a monochromatic <a href="/wiki/Plane_wave" title="Plane wave">plane wave</a> of optical frequency <i>f</i> (light of vacuum wavelength &#955; has a frequency of <i>f = c/&#955;</i> where <i>c</i> is the speed of light), let us take the direction of propagation as the <i>z</i> axis. Being a transverse wave the <b>E</b> and <b>H</b> fields must then contain components only in the <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> directions whereas <i>E<sub>z</sub> = H<sub>z</sub> = 0</i>. Using <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Phasor" title="Phasor">phasor</a>) notation, the instantaneous physical electric and magnetic fields are given by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_part" title="Real part">real parts</a> of the complex quantities occurring in the following equations. As a function of time <i>t</i> and spatial position <i>z</i> (since for a plane wave in the +<i>z</i> direction the fields have no dependence on <i>x</i> or <i>y</i>) these complex fields can be written as:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\vec {E}}(z,t)={\\begin{bmatrix}e_{x}\\\\e_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i2\\pi \\left({\\frac {z}{\\lambda }}-{\\frac {t}{T}}\\right)}={\\begin{bmatrix}e_{x}\\\\e_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i(kz-\\omega t)}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mi>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mi>T</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow>\n<mo>[</mo>\n<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>y</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n</mtable>\n<mo>]</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>&#969;<!-- &#969; --></mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\vec {E}}(z,t)={\\begin{bmatrix}e_{x}\\\\e_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i2\\pi \\left({\\frac {z}{\\lambda }}-{\\frac {t}{T}}\\right)}={\\begin{bmatrix}e_{x}\\\\e_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i(kz-\\omega t)}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\vec {E}}(z,t)={\\begin{bmatrix}e_{x}\\\\e_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i2\\pi \\left({\\frac {z}{\\lambda }}-{\\frac {t}{T}}\\right)}={\\begin{bmatrix}e_{x}\\\\e_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i(kz-\\omega t)}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4326f994848db60d4e85ff10f9f6298e69101c90" style="vertical-align: -4.171ex; width:44.211ex; height:9.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>and\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\vec {H}}(z,t)={\\begin{bmatrix}h_{x}\\\\h_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i2\\pi \\left({\\frac {z}{\\lambda }}-{\\frac {t}{T}}\\right)}={\\begin{bmatrix}h_{x}\\\\h_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i(kz-\\omega t)}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mo>\n<mi>&#969;<!-- &#969; --></mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\vec {H}}(z,t)={\\begin{bmatrix}h_{x}\\\\h_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i2\\pi \\left({\\frac {z}{\\lambda }}-{\\frac {t}{T}}\\right)}={\\begin{bmatrix}h_{x}\\\\h_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i(kz-\\omega t)}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\vec {H}}(z,t)={\\begin{bmatrix}h_{x}\\\\h_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i2\\pi \\left({\\frac {z}{\\lambda }}-{\\frac {t}{T}}\\right)}={\\begin{bmatrix}h_{x}\\\\h_{y}\\\\0\\end{bmatrix}}\\;e^{i(kz-\\omega t)}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c1ff79468d0aa2ba26cbe4e92f5ec08dd23edbc7" style="vertical-align: -4.171ex; width:45.01ex; height:9.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where &#955; = &#955;<sub>0</sub>/<i>n</i> is the wavelength <i>in the medium</i> (whose <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> is <i>n</i>) and <span class="nowrap"><i>T</i> = 1/<i>f</i></span> is the period of the wave. Here <i>e<sub>x</sub></i>, <i>e<sub>y</sub></i>, <i>h<sub>x</sub></i>, and <i>h<sub>y</sub></i> are complex numbers. In the second more compact form, as these equations are customarily expressed, these factors are described using the  <a href="/wiki/Wavenumber" title="Wavenumber">wavenumber</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle k=2\\pi n/\\lambda }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>&#960;<!-- &#960; --></mi>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle k=2\\pi n/\\lambda }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle k=2\\pi n/\\lambda }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ef85afd1d9bb29207acae41bddf6715650ac9362" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:10.717ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> and <a href="/wiki/Angular_frequency" title="Angular frequency">angular frequency</a> (or "radian frequency") <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\omega =2\\pi f}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#969;<!-- &#969; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>&#960;<!-- &#960; --></mi>\n<mi>f</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\omega =2\\pi f}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\omega =2\\pi f" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7a1bf35d395c2d52391265e4bbda0aed14f52579" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.317ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>. In a more general formulation with propagation <i>not</i> restricted to the <i>+z</i> direction, then the spatial dependence <i>kz</i> is replaced by <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\vec {k}}\\cdot {\\vec {r}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\vec {k}}\\cdot {\\vec {r}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\vec{k} \\cdot \\vec{r}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bffe6002b1ce9a2f0f1414966427aa50ab8a93e3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:4.113ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\vec {k}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\vec {k}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\vec {k}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5ccd4b98d198d6538010ae815ee1199baabd3493" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.211ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is called the <a href="/wiki/Wave_vector" title="Wave vector">wave vector</a>, the magnitude of which is the wavenumber.\n</p><p>Thus the leading vectors <b>e</b> and <b>h</b> each contain up to two nonzero (complex) components describing the amplitude and phase of the wave\'s  <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> polarization components (again, there can be no <i>z</i> polarization component for a transverse wave in the +<i>z</i> direction). For a given medium with a <a href="/wiki/Wave_impedance" title="Wave impedance">characteristic impedance</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\eta }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#951;<!-- &#951; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\eta }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\eta " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e4d701857cf5fbec133eebaf94deadf722537f64" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.169ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, <b>h</b> is related to <b>e</b> by:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle h_{y}={\\frac {e_{x}}{\\eta }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>y</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>&#951;<!-- &#951; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle h_{y}={\\frac {e_{x}}{\\eta }}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle h_{y}={\\frac {e_{x}}{\\eta }}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6c3ea89b76d79d4f9514e2ff5dda7f469814ae5f" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:8.579ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>and\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle h_{x}=-{\\frac {e_{y}}{\\eta }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>y</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>&#951;<!-- &#951; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle h_{x}=-{\\frac {e_{y}}{\\eta }}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle h_{x}=-{\\frac {e_{y}}{\\eta }}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f10f8e2c6f61eb4493913272a048eeee6f796747" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:10.387ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span>.</dd></dl>\n<p>In a dielectric, <i>&#951;</i> is real and has the value <i>&#951;</i><sub>0</sub>/<i>n</i>, where <i>n</i> is the refractive index and <i>&#951;</i><sub>0</sub> is the impedance of free space. The impedance will be complex in a conducting medium.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="But if eta is not real, how can we complete the proof of orthogonality? Don\'t we take real parts before doing the dot product? See talk page. (May 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Note that given that relationship, the dot product of <b>E</b> and <b>H</b> must be zero:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (May 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Polarization_(waves)#Dubious" title="Talk:Polarization (waves)">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\vec {E}}\\left({\\vec {r}},t\\right)\\cdot {\\vec {H}}\\left({\\vec {r}},t\\right)=e_{x}h_{x}+e_{y}h_{y}+e_{z}h_{z}=e_{x}\\left(-{\\frac {e_{y}}{\\eta }}\\right)+e_{y}\\left({\\frac {e_{x}}{\\eta }}\\right)+0\\cdot 0=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>y</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>y</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>z</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>z</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>y</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>&#951;<!-- &#951; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>y</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>&#951;<!-- &#951; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\vec {E}}\\left({\\vec {r}},t\\right)\\cdot {\\vec {H}}\\left({\\vec {r}},t\\right)=e_{x}h_{x}+e_{y}h_{y}+e_{z}h_{z}=e_{x}\\left(-{\\frac {e_{y}}{\\eta }}\\right)+e_{y}\\left({\\frac {e_{x}}{\\eta }}\\right)+0\\cdot 0=0}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\vec {E}}\\left({\\vec {r}},t\\right)\\cdot {\\vec {H}}\\left({\\vec {r}},t\\right)=e_{x}h_{x}+e_{y}h_{y}+e_{z}h_{z}=e_{x}\\left(-{\\frac {e_{y}}{\\eta }}\\right)+e_{y}\\left({\\frac {e_{x}}{\\eta }}\\right)+0\\cdot 0=0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cd31e11399e45c0faceae7d30ab9bfb38035dfc1" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:75.517ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>indicating that these vectors are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthogonal" title="Orthogonal">orthogonal</a> (at right angles to each other), as expected.\n</p><p>So knowing the propagation direction (+<i>z</i> in this case) and &#951;, one can just as well specify the wave in terms of just <i>e<sub>x</sub></i> and <i>e<sub>y</sub></i> describing the electric field. The vector containing <i>e<sub>x</sub></i> and <i>e<sub>y</sub></i> (but without the <i>z</i> component which is necessarily zero for a transverse wave) is known as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jones_vector" title="Jones vector">Jones vector</a>. In addition to specifying the polarization state of the wave, a general Jones vector also specifies the overall magnitude and phase of that wave. Specifically, the <a href="/wiki/Intensity_(physics)" title="Intensity (physics)">intensity</a> of the light wave is proportional to the sum of the squared magnitudes of the two electric field components:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle I=\\left(\\left|e_{x}\\right|^{2}+\\left|e_{y}\\right|^{2}\\right)\\,{\\frac {1}{2\\eta }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>I</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>|</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>|</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>y</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>&#951;<!-- &#951; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle I=\\left(\\left|e_{x}\\right|^{2}+\\left|e_{y}\\right|^{2}\\right)\\,{\\frac {1}{2\\eta }}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle I=\\left(\\left|e_{x}\\right|^{2}+\\left|e_{y}\\right|^{2}\\right)\\,{\\frac {1}{2\\eta }}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/654a9a8f373dbcaa635740bfef5dba1109aa4efa" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:22.913ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>however the wave\'s <i>state of polarization</i> is only dependent on the (complex) <i>ratio</i> of <i>e<sub>y</sub></i> to <i>e<sub>x</sub></i>. So let us just consider waves whose <i>|e<sub>x</sub>|<sup>2</sup> + |e<sub>y</sub>|<sup>2</sup> = 1</i>; this happens to correspond to an intensity of about .00133 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> per square meter in free space (where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\eta =}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#951;<!-- &#951; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\eta =}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\eta = " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/245c0732e8a68a877b2dbb29c58b3bc947fdfe16" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.623ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> <a href="/wiki/Impedance_of_free_space" title="Impedance of free space"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\eta _{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#951;<!-- &#951; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\eta _{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\eta _{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ef3886c3f161b7f02d23221bfead21a18ffb82ff" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:2.21ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span></a>). And since the absolute phase of a wave is unimportant in discussing its polarization state, let us stipulate that the phase of <i>e<sub>x</sub></i> is zero, in other words <i>e<sub>x</sub></i> is a real number while <i>e<sub>y</sub></i> may be complex. Under these restrictions, <i>e<sub>x</sub></i> and <i>e<sub>y</sub></i> can be represented as follows:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle e_{x}={\\sqrt {\\frac {1+Q}{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>Q</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle e_{x}={\\sqrt {\\frac {1+Q}{2}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle e_{x}={\\sqrt {\\frac {1+Q}{2}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7b4396b72cd203f536a71b6539c11728dc056036" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:14.356ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle e_{y}={\\sqrt {\\frac {1-Q}{2}}}\\,e^{i\\phi }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>y</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>Q</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle e_{y}={\\sqrt {\\frac {1-Q}{2}}}\\,e^{i\\phi }}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle e_{y}={\\sqrt {\\frac {1-Q}{2}}}\\,e^{i\\phi }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6f77f347ee559f13c8f375bc98cd3cc4b9a393ce" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:17.482ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where the polarization state is now fully parameterized by the value of <i>Q</i> (such that &#8722;1 &lt; <i>Q</i> &lt; 1) and the relative phase <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\phi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\phi }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\phi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/72b1f30316670aee6270a28334bdf4f5072cdde4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.385ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-transverse_waves">Non-transverse waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Non-transverse waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In addition to transverse waves, there are many wave motions where the oscillation is not limited to directions perpendicular to the direction of propagation. These cases are far beyond the scope of the current article which concentrates on transverse waves (such as most electromagnetic waves in bulk media), however one should be aware of cases where the polarization of a coherent wave cannot be described simply using a Jones vector, as we have just done.\n</p><p>Just considering electromagnetic waves, we note that the preceding discussion strictly applies to plane waves in a homogeneous isotropic non-attenuating medium, whereas in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anisotropic" title="Anisotropic">anisotropic</a> medium (such as birefringent crystals as discussed below) the electric or magnetic field may have longitudinal as well as transverse components. In those cases the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_displacement" title="Electric displacement">electric displacement</a> <i>D</i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_flux_density" title="Magnetic flux density">magnetic flux density</a> <i>B</i><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="But B doesn\'t appear above. (May 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> still obey the above geometry but due to anisotropy in the <a href="/wiki/Electric_susceptibility" title="Electric susceptibility">electric susceptibility</a> (or in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_permeability" title="Magnetic permeability">magnetic permeability</a>), now given by a <a href="/wiki/Tensor" title="Tensor">tensor</a>, the direction of <i>E</i> (or <i>H</i>) may differ from that of <i>D</i> (or <i>B</i>). Even in isotropic media, so-called <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Inhomogeneous_wave&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Inhomogeneous wave (page does not exist)">inhomogeneous waves</a> can be launched into a medium whose refractive index has a significant imaginary part (or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_index_of_refraction#Complex_index_of_refraction_and_absorption" title="Complex index of refraction">extinction coefficient</a>") such as metals;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Is this the same as having a complex value of the wave impedance &#951; that was employed in the previous section? The discussion should be made consistent and the assumptions on the reality of &#951; (or not) should be made explicit. (May 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> these fields are also not strictly transverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffiths1998_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Griffiths1998-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>179&#8211;184</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New2011_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-New2011-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>51&#8211;52</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Surface_wave" title="Surface wave">Surface waves</a> or waves propagating in a <a href="/wiki/Waveguide" title="Waveguide">waveguide</a> (such as an <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">optical fiber</a>) are generally <i>not</i> transverse waves, but might be described as an electric or magnetic <a href="/wiki/Transverse_mode" title="Transverse mode">transverse mode</a>, or a hybrid mode.\n</p><p>Even in free space, longitudinal field components can be generated in focal regions, where the plane wave approximation breaks down. An extreme example is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radial_polarisation" title="Radial polarisation">radially</a> or tangentially polarized light, at the focus of which the electric or magnetic field respectively is <i>entirely</i> longitudinal (along the direction of propagation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For <a href="/wiki/Longitudinal_wave" title="Longitudinal wave">longitudinal waves</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_wave" title="Sound wave">sound waves</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluids</a>, the direction of oscillation is by definition along the direction of travel, so the issue of polarization is not normally even mentioned. On the other hand, sound waves in a bulk <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a> can be transverse as well as longitudinal, for a total of three polarization components. In this case, the transverse polarization is associated with the direction of the <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">shear stress</a> and displacement in directions perpendicular to the propagation direction, while the longitudinal polarization describes compression of the solid and vibration along the direction of propagation. The differential propagation of transverse and longitudinal polarizations is important in <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarization_state">Polarization state<span id="State"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Polarization state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Linear_polarization" title="Linear polarization">Linear polarization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">Circular polarization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_polarization" title="Elliptical polarization">Elliptical polarization</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:62px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarisation_rectiligne.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="237" data-file-width="96" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Polarisation_rectiligne.gif/60px-Polarisation_rectiligne.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Polarisation_rectiligne.gif/90px-Polarisation_rectiligne.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Polarisation_rectiligne.gif 2x" width="60"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polarisation_rectiligne.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Electric field oscillation</div></div></div>\n<p>Polarization is best understood by initially considering only pure polarization states, and only a coherent sinusoidal wave at some optical frequency. The vector in the adjacent diagram might describe the oscillation of the electric field emitted by a single-mode laser (whose oscillation frequency would be typically 10<sup>15</sup> times faster). The field oscillates in the <i>x-y</i> plane, along the page, with the wave propagating in the <i>z</i> direction, perpendicular to the page.\nThe first two diagrams below trace the electric field vector over a complete cycle for linear polarization at two different orientations; these are each considered a distinct <i>state of polarization</i> (SOP). Note that the linear polarization at 45&#176; can also be viewed as the addition of a horizontally linearly polarized wave (as in the leftmost figure) and a vertically polarized wave of the same amplitude <i>in the same phase</i>.\n</p>\n<div style="float:left;width:100px">\n<div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarisation_state_-_Linear_polarization_parallel_to_x_axis.svg"><img alt="Polarisation state - Linear polarization parallel to x axis.svg" data-file-height="125" 2x" width="100"/></a></div></div>\n</div>\n<div style="float:left;width:100px">\n<p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarisation_state_-_Right-elliptical_polarization_A.svg"><img alt="Polarisation state - Right-elliptical polarization A.svg" data-file-height="125" data-file-width="125" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Polarisation_state_-_Right-elliptical_polarization_A.svg/100px-Polarisation_state_-_Right-elliptical_polarization_A.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Polarisation_state_-_Right-elliptical_polarization_A.svg/150px-Polarisation_state_-_Right-elliptical_polarization_A.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Polarisation_state_-_Right-elliptical_polarization_A.svg/200px-Polarisation_state_-_Right-elliptical_polarization_A.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a>\n</p>\n</div>\n<div style="float:left;width:100px">\n<p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarisation_state_-_Right-circular_polarization.svg"><img alt="Polarisation state - Right-circular polarization.svg" data-file-height="125" data-file-width="125" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Polarisation_state_-_Right-circular_polarization.svg/100px-Polarisation_state_-_Right-circular_polarization.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Polarisation_state_-_Right-circular_polarization.svg/150px-Polarisation_state_-_Right-circular_polarization.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Polarisation_state_-_Right-circular_polarization.svg/200px-Polarisation_state_-_Right-circular_polarization.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a>\n</p>\n</div>\n<div style="float:left;width:100px">\n<p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarisation_state_-_Left-circular_polarization.svg"><img alt="Polarisation state - Left-circular polarization.svg" data-file-height="125" data-file-width="125" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Polarisation_state_-_Left-circular_polarization.svg/100px-Polarisation_state_-_Left-circular_polarization.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Polarisation_state_-_Left-circular_polarization.svg/150px-Polarisation_state_-_Left-circular_polarization.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Polarisation_state_-_Left-circular_polarization.svg/200px-Polarisation_state_-_Left-circular_polarization.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a>\n</p>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_Polarisation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="400" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Wave_Polarisation.gif/200px-Wave_Polarisation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/Wave_Polarisation.gif 1.5x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_Polarisation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation showing four different polarization states and two orthogonal projections.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rising_circular.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Rising_circular.gif/200px-Rising_circular.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Rising_circular.gif/300px-Rising_circular.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Rising_circular.gif/400px-Rising_circular.gif 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rising_circular.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A circularly polarized wave as a sum of two linearly polarized components 90&#176; out of phase</div></div></div>\n<p>Now if one were to introduce a <a href="/wiki/Phase_(waves)#phase_shift" title="Phase (waves)">phase shift</a> in between those horizontal and vertical polarization components, one would generally obtain elliptical polarization<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>  as is shown in the third figure. When the phase shift is exactly &#177;90&#176;, then <i>circular polarization</i> is produced (fourth and fifth figures). Thus is circular polarization created in practice, starting with linearly polarized light and employing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quarter-wave_plate" title="Quarter-wave plate">quarter-wave plate</a> to introduce such a phase shift. The result of two such phase-shifted components in causing a rotating electric field vector is depicted in the animation on the right. Note that circular or elliptical polarization can involve either a clockwise or counterclockwise rotation of the field. These correspond  to distinct polarization states, such as the two circular polarizations shown above.\n</p><p>Of course the orientation of the <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> axes used in this description is arbitrary. The choice of such a coordinate system and viewing the polarization ellipse in terms of the  <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> polarization components, corresponds to the definition of the Jones vector (below) in terms of those <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basis_functions" title="Basis functions">basis</a> polarizations. One would typically choose axes to suit a particular problem such as <i>x</i> being in the plane of incidence. Since there are separate reflection coefficients for the linear polarizations in and orthogonal to the plane of incidence (<i>p</i> and <i>s</i> polarizations, see below), that choice greatly simplifies the calculation of a wave\'s reflection from a surface.\n</p><p>Moreover, one can use as basis functions <i>any</i> pair of <a href="/wiki/Orthogonality" title="Orthogonality">orthogonal</a> polarization states, not just linear polarizations. For instance, choosing right and left circular polarizations as basis functions simplifies the solution of problems involving circular birefringence (optical activity) or circular dichroism.\n</p><p><span id="Parameterization"></span>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarization_ellipse">Polarization ellipse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Polarization ellipse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polarization_ellipse" title="Polarization ellipse">Polarization ellipse</a></div>\n<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarisation_ellipse2.svg"><img alt="Polarisation ellipse2.svg" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Polarisation_ellipse2.svg/250px-Polarisation_ellipse2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Polarisation_ellipse2.svg/375px-Polarisation_ellipse2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Polarisation_ellipse2.svg/500px-Polarisation_ellipse2.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a></div>\n<p>Consider a purely polarized monochromatic wave. If one were to plot the electric field vector over one cycle of oscillation, an ellipse would generally be obtained, as is shown in the figure, corresponding to a particular state of <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_polarization" title="Elliptical polarization">elliptical polarization</a>. Note that linear polarization and circular polarization can be seen as special cases of elliptical polarization.\n</p><p>A polarization state can then be described in relation to the geometrical parameters of the ellipse, and its "handedness", that is, whether the rotation around the ellipse is clockwise or counter clockwise. One parameterization of the elliptical figure specifies the <b>orientation angle</b> &#968;, defined as the angle between the major axis of the ellipse and the <i>x</i>-axis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stetten_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stetten-13">[13]</a></sup> along with the <b>ellipticity</b> &#949;=a/b, the ratio of the ellipse\'s major to minor axis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Axial_ratio" title="Axial ratio">axial ratio</a>). The ellipticity parameter is an alternative parameterization of an ellipse\'s <a href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(mathematics)#Ellipses" title="Eccentricity (mathematics)">eccentricity</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle e={\\sqrt {1-b^{2}/a^{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>b</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle e={\\sqrt {1-b^{2}/a^{2}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle e={\\sqrt {1-b^{2}/a^{2}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3ca82122c1c9b8997445bf26272baa1e797206e1" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; width:16.007ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span>, or the <b>ellipticity angle</b>, &#967;&#160;=&#160;arctan&#160;b/a=&#160;arctan&#160;1/&#949; as is shown in the figure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stetten_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stetten-13">[13]</a></sup> The angle &#967; is also significant in that the latitude (angle from the equator) of the polarization state as represented on the Poincar&#233; sphere (see below) is equal to &#177;2&#967;. The special cases of linear and circular polarization correspond to an ellipticity &#949; of infinity and unity (or &#967; of zero and 45&#176;) respectively.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jones_vector">Jones vector</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Jones vector">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jones_vector" title="Jones vector">Jones vector</a></div>\n<p>Full information on a completely polarized state is also provided by the amplitude and phase of oscillations in two components of the electric field vector in the plane of polarization. This representation was used above to show how different states of polarization are possible. The amplitude and phase information can be conveniently represented as a two-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex</a> vector (the <a href="/wiki/Jones_calculus" title="Jones calculus">Jones vector</a>):\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {e} ={\\begin{bmatrix}a_{1}e^{i\\theta _{1}}\\\\a_{2}e^{i\\theta _{2}}\\end{bmatrix}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">e</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow>\n<mo>[</mo>\n<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<msub>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<msub>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n</mtable>\n<mo>]</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {e} ={\\begin{bmatrix}a_{1}e^{i\\theta _{1}}\\\\a_{2}e^{i\\theta _{2}}\\end{bmatrix}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {e} ={\\begin{bmatrix}a_{1}e^{i\\theta _{1}}\\\\a_{2}e^{i\\theta _{2}}\\end{bmatrix}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6c813c95bdf40d43a2e68445040ff6bf8629be4f" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:13.947ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Here <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle a_{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle a_{1}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="a_{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bbf42ecda092975c9c69dae84e16182ba5fe2e07" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.284ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle a_{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle a_{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="a_{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/270580da7333505d9b73697417d0543c43c98b9f" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.284ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> denote the amplitude of the wave in the two components of the electric field vector, while <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\theta _{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\theta _{1}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\theta _{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7f84b9443d095623e02fd287cd095123d70b0278" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.145ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\theta _{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\theta _{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\theta _{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0ed6ea624b20b153403979ffaf5434fc36de2990" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.145ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> represent the phases. The product of a Jones vector with a complex number of unit <a href="/wiki/Absolute_value" title="Absolute value">modulus</a> gives a different Jones vector representing the same ellipse, and thus the same state of polarization. The physical electric field, as the real part of the Jones vector, would be altered but the polarization state itself is independent of <a href="/wiki/Absolute_phase" title="Absolute phase">absolute phase</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Basis_(linear_algebra)" title="Basis (linear algebra)">basis</a> vectors used to represent the Jones vector need not represent linear polarization states (i.e. be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_numbers" title="Real numbers">real</a>). In general any two <b>orthogonal states</b> can be used, where an orthogonal vector pair is formally defined as one having a zero <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_product" title="Inner product">inner product</a>. A common choice is left and right circular polarizations, for example to model the different propagation of waves in two such components in circularly birefringent media (see below) or signal paths of coherent detectors sensitive to circular polarization.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordinate_frame">Coordinate frame</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Coordinate frame">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Regardless of whether polarization state is represented using geometric parameters or Jones vectors, implicit in the parameterization is the orientation of the coordinate frame. This permits a degree of freedom, namely rotation about the propagation direction. When considering light that is propagating parallel to the surface of the Earth, the terms "horizontal" and "vertical" polarization are often used, with the former being associated with the first component of the Jones vector, or zero azimuth angle. On the other hand, in <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> the <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_coordinate_system" title="Equatorial coordinate system">equatorial coordinate system</a> is generally used instead, with the zero azimuth (or position angle, as it is more commonly called in astronomy to avoid confusion with the <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_coordinate_system" title="Horizontal coordinate system">horizontal coordinate system</a>) corresponding to due north.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="s_and_p_designations"><i>s</i> and <i>p</i> designations<span id="s_and_p"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: s and p designations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:250px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:640px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/E_xy_deformation.webm/640px--E_xy_deformation.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:640px;height:406px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="30.033" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="E_xy_deformation.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/E_xy_deformation.webm" type="video/webm; width:11.941ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> along with 3 planar projections and a deformation surface of total electric field. The light is always s-polarized in the xy plane. <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\textstyle \\theta }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\textstyle \\theta }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\textstyle \\theta }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a11744bd71a5eb6efe4f28e12ca57f874d82658c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.09ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the polar angle of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\textstyle {\\textbf {k}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext mathvariant="bold">k</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\textstyle {\\textbf {k}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\textstyle {\\textbf {k}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0ff5c476a4abf6ead9687409c42280f4e3669e7b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.411ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\textstyle \\varphi _{E}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#966;<!-- &#966; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\textstyle \\varphi _{E}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\textstyle \\varphi _{E}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/71c4125c3725f4a0838e45b26d3f48b915f442a6" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.008ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the azimuthal angle of  <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\textstyle {\\textbf {E}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext mathvariant="bold">E</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\textstyle {\\textbf {E}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\textstyle {\\textbf {E}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d67f2d6c251108e8556a157270d0f67a63f58ed7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.757ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Another coordinate system frequently used relates to the <i><a href="/wiki/Plane_of_incidence" title="Plane of incidence">plane of incidence</a></i>. This is the plane made by the incoming propagation direction and the vector perpendicular to the plane of an interface, in other words, the plane in which the ray travels before and after reflection or refraction.  The component of the electric field parallel to this plane is termed <i>p-like</i> (parallel) and the component perpendicular to this plane is termed <i>s-like</i> (from <i>senkrecht</i>, German for perpendicular). Polarized light with its electric field along the plane of incidence is thus denoted <i>p-polarized</i>, while light whose electric field is normal to the plane of incidence is called <i>s-polarized</i>. <i>P</i> polarization is commonly referred to as <i>transverse-magnetic</i> (TM), and has also been termed <i>pi-polarized</i> or <i>tangential plane polarized</i>. <i>S</i> polarization is also called <i>transverse-electric</i> (TE), as well as <i>sigma-polarized</i> or <i>sagittal plane polarized</i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unpolarized_and_partially_polarized_light">Unpolarized and partially polarized light</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Unpolarized and partially polarized light">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="box-Repetition_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may contain content that is repetitive or redundant of text elsewhere in the article</b>. Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by merging similar text or removing repeated statements. <span style="font-size:85%;"><i>(July 2014)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Natural light, and most other common sources of visible light, are <a href="/wiki/Coherence_(physics)" title="Coherence (physics)">incoherent</a>: radiation is produced independently by a large number of atoms or molecules whose emissions are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statistical_correlation" title="Statistical correlation">uncorrelated</a> and generally of random polarizations. In this case the light is said to be <i>unpolarized</i>. This term is somewhat inexact, since at any instant of time at one location there is a definite direction to the electric and magnetic fields, however it implies that the polarization changes so quickly in time that it will not be measured or relevant to the outcome of an experiment.  A so-called <a href="/wiki/Depolarizer_(optics)" title="Depolarizer (optics)">depolarizer</a> acts on a polarized beam to create one which is actually <i>fully</i> polarized at every point, but in which the polarization varies so rapidly across the beam that it may be ignored in the intended applications.\n</p><p>Unpolarized light can be described as a mixture of two independent oppositely polarized streams, each with half the intensity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Light is said to be <i>partially polarized</i> when there is more power in one of these streams than the other. At any particular wavelength, partially polarized light can be statistically described as the superposition of a completely unpolarized component and a completely polarized one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hecht2002_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hecht2002-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>330</span></sup> One may then describe the light in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_polarization" title="Degree of polarization">degree of polarization</a> and the parameters of the polarized component. That polarized component can be described in terms of a Jones vector or polarization ellipse, as is detailed above. However, in order to also describe the degree of polarization, one normally employs Stokes parameters (see below) to specify a state of partial polarization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hecht2002_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hecht2002-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>351,374&#8211;375</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Motivation">Motivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Motivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The transmission of plane waves through a homogeneous medium are fully described in terms of Jones vectors and 2&#215;2 Jones matrices. However, in practice there are cases in which all of the light cannot be viewed in such a simple manner due to spatial inhomogeneities or the presence of mutually incoherent waves. So-called depolarization, for instance, cannot be described using Jones matrices. For these cases it is usual instead to use a 4&#215;4 matrix that acts upon the Stokes 4-vector. Such matrices were first used by Paul Soleillet in 1929, although they have come to be known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mueller_matrix" title="Mueller matrix">Mueller matrices</a>. While every Jones matrix has a Mueller matrix, the reverse is not true. Mueller matrices are then used to describe the observed polarization effects of the <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">scattering</a> of waves from complex surfaces or ensembles of particles, as shall now be presented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hecht2002_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hecht2002-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>377&#8211;379</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coherency_matrix">Coherency matrix</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Coherency matrix">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Jones vector perfectly describes the state of polarization <i>and phase</i> of a single monochromatic wave, representing a pure state of polarization as described above. However any mixture of waves of different polarizations (or even of different frequencies) do <i>not</i> correspond to a Jones vector. In so-called partially polarized radiation the fields are <a href="/wiki/Stochastic" title="Stochastic">stochastic</a>, and the variations and correlations between components of the electric field can only be described <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statistical" title="Statistical">statistically</a>. One such representation is the <b>coherency <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)" title="Matrix (mathematics)">matrix</a></b>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O\'Neill2004_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-O\'Neill2004-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>137&#8211;142</span></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}\\mathbf {\\Psi } &amp;=\\left\\langle \\mathbf {e} \\mathbf {e} ^{\\dagger }\\right\\rangle \\\\&amp;=\\left\\langle {\\begin{bmatrix}e_{1}e_{1}^{*}&amp;e_{1}e_{2}^{*}\\\\e_{2}e_{1}^{*}&amp;e_{2}e_{2}^{*}\\end{bmatrix}}\\right\\rangle \\\\&amp;=\\left\\langle {\\begin{bmatrix}a_{1}^{2}&amp;a_{1}a_{2}e^{i\\left(\\theta _{1}-\\theta _{2}\\right)}\\\\a_{1}a_{2}e^{-i\\left(\\theta _{1}-\\theta _{2}\\right)}&amp;a_{2}^{2}\\end{bmatrix}}\\right\\rangle \\end{aligned}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true" rowspacing="3pt">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">&#936;<!-- &#936;
