Partially polarized states will lie <i>inside</i> the Poincar&#233; sphere at a distance of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle P\'={\\sqrt {S_{1}\'^{2}+S_{2}\'^{2}+S_{3}\'^{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>P</mi>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mo class="MJX-variant">&#8242;</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msubsup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mo class="MJX-variant">&#8242;</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msubsup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mo class="MJX-variant">&#8242;</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msubsup>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle P\'={\\sqrt {S_{1}\'^{2}+S_{2}\'^{2}+S_{3}\'^{2}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle P\'={\\sqrt {S_{1}\'^{2}+S_{2}\'^{2}+S_{3}\'^{2}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/36405b776a677e57fb63251666b66cb24e2730f4" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:22.694ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span> from the origin. When the non-polarized component is not of interest, the Stokes vector can be further normalized to obtain \n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {S\'\'} ={\\frac {1}{P\'}}{\\begin{bmatrix}1\\\\S\'_{1}\\\\S\'_{2}\\\\S\'_{3}\\end{bmatrix}}={\\frac {1}{P}}{\\begin{bmatrix}S_{0}\\\\S_{1}\\\\S_{2}\\\\S_{3}\\end{bmatrix}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msup>\n<mi mathvariant="bold">S</mi>\n<mo>&#8243;</mo>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<msup>\n<mi>P</mi>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow>\n<mo>[</mo>\n<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msubsup>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msubsup>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msubsup>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n</mtable>\n<mo>]</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mi>P</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow>\n<mo>[</mo>\n<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n</mtable>\n<mo>]</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {S\'\'} ={\\frac {1}{P\'}}{\\begin{bmatrix}1\\\\S\'_{1}\\\\S\'_{2}\\\\S\'_{3}\\end{bmatrix}}={\\frac {1}{P}}{\\begin{bmatrix}S_{0}\\\\S_{1}\\\\S_{2}\\\\S_{3}\\end{bmatrix}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {S\'\'} ={\\frac {1}{P\'}}{\\begin{bmatrix}1\\\\S\'_{1}\\\\S\'_{2}\\\\S\'_{3}\\end{bmatrix}}={\\frac {1}{P}}{\\begin{bmatrix}S_{0}\\\\S_{1}\\\\S_{2}\\\\S_{3}\\end{bmatrix}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0449b5f7119e71fa4ca1a9e59af43a2f3b1b523d" style="vertical-align: -5.851ex; margin-bottom: -0.32ex; width:28.053ex; height:13.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>When plotted, that point will lie on the surface of the unity-radius Poincar&#233; sphere and indicate the state of polarization of the polarized component.\n</p><p>Any two antipodal points on the Poincar&#233; sphere refer to orthogonal polarization states. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_product" title="Inner product">overlap</a> between any two polarization states is dependent solely on the distance between their locations along the sphere. This property, which can only be true when pure polarization states are mapped onto a sphere, is the motivation for the invention of the Poincar&#233; sphere and the use of Stokes parameters, which are thus plotted on (or beneath) it.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Implications_for_reflection_and_propagation">Implications for reflection and propagation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Implications for reflection and propagation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarization_in_wave_propagation">Polarization in wave propagation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Polarization in wave propagation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>, the components of the electric field propagate at the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>, so that the phase of the wave varies in space and time while the polarization state does not. That is, the electric field vector <b>e</b> of a plane wave in the +<i>z</i> direction follows:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {e} (z+\\Delta z,t+\\Delta t)=\\mathbf {e} (z,t)e^{ik(c\\Delta t-\\Delta z)},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">e</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">e</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {e} (z+\\Delta z,t+\\Delta t)=\\mathbf {e} (z,t)e^{ik(c\\Delta t-\\Delta z)},}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {e} (z+\\Delta z,t+\\Delta t)=\\mathbf {e} (z,t)e^{ik(c\\Delta t-\\Delta z)},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bc071d76770c99ec9fd9102d9546b9ced4c6749f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:37.328ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <i>k</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Wavenumber" title="Wavenumber">wavenumber</a>. As noted above, the instantaneous electric field is the real part of the product of the Jones vector times the phase factor <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle e^{-i\\omega t}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>&#969;<!-- &#969; --></mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle e^{-i\\omega t}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="e^{{-i\\omega t}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7c4bab37784e0c6bedf7e592b763d6aa59266c92" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:4.778ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>. When an electromagnetic wave interacts with matter, its propagation is altered according to the material\'s (complex) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Index_of_refraction" title="Index of refraction">index of refraction</a>. When the real or imaginary part of that refractive index is dependent on the polarization state of a wave, properties known as <a href="/wiki/Birefringence" title="Birefringence">birefringence</a> and polarization <a href="/wiki/Dichroism" title="Dichroism">dichroism</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diattenuator" title="Diattenuator">diattenuation</a>) respectively, then the polarization state of a wave will generally be altered.\n</p><p>In such media, an electromagnetic wave with any given state of polarization may be decomposed into two orthogonally polarized components that encounter different <a href="/wiki/Propagation_constant" title="Propagation constant">propagation constants</a>. The effect of propagation over a given path on those two components is most easily characterized in the form of a complex 2&#215;2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_transformation" title="Linear transformation">transformation</a> matrix <b>J</b> known as a <a href="/wiki/Jones_calculus" title="Jones calculus">Jones matrix</a>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {e\'} =\\mathbf {J} \\mathbf {e} .}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msup>\n<mi mathvariant="bold">e</mi>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">J</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">e</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {e\'} =\\mathbf {J} \\mathbf {e} .}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mathbf{e\'} = \\mathbf{J}\\mathbf{e}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3309df37f76778812a978c4296d2a6a299cab913" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.261ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The Jones matrix due to passage through a transparent material is dependent on the propagation distance as well as the birefringence. The birefringence (as well as the average refractive index) will generally be <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">dispersive</a>, that is, it will vary as a function of optical frequency (wavelength). In the case of non-birefringent materials, however, the 2&#215;2 Jones matrix is the identity matrix (multiplied by a scalar <a href="/wiki/Phase_factor" title="Phase factor">phase factor</a> and attenuation factor), implying no change in polarization during propagation.\n</p><p>For propagation effects in two orthogonal modes, the Jones matrix can be written as\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {J} =\\mathbf {T} {\\begin{bmatrix}g_{1}&amp;0\\\\0&amp;g_{2}\\end{bmatrix}}\\mathbf {T} ^{-1},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">J</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">T</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow>\n<mo>[</mo>\n<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n<mtd>\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mtd>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n</mtable>\n<mo>]</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">T</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {J} =\\mathbf {T} {\\begin{bmatrix}g_{1}&amp;0\\\\0&amp;g_{2}\\end{bmatrix}}\\mathbf {T} ^{-1},}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {J} =\\mathbf {T} {\\begin{bmatrix}g_{1}&amp;0\\\\0&amp;g_{2}\\end{bmatrix}}\\mathbf {T} ^{-1},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/761f10d55cabb915c289eed41c5e345426dbc1d0" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:21.033ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <i>g</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>g</i><sub>2</sub> are complex numbers\ndescribing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phase_delay" title="Phase delay">phase delay</a> and possibly the amplitude attenuation due to propagation in each of the two polarization eigenmodes. <b>T</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_matrix" title="Unitary matrix">unitary matrix</a> representing a change of basis from these propagation modes to the linear system used for the Jones vectors; in the case of linear birefringence or diattenuation the modes are themselves linear polarization states so <b>T</b> and <b>T<sup>&#8722;1</sup></b> can be omitted if the coordinate axes have been chosen appropriately.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Birefringence">Birefringence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Birefringence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Birefringence" title="Birefringence">Birefringence</a></div>\n<p>In media termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birefringent" title="Birefringent">birefringent</a>, in which the amplitudes are unchanged but a differential phase delay occurs, the Jones matrix is a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_matrix" title="Unitary matrix">unitary matrix</a>: |<i>g</i><sub>1</sub>| = |<i>g</i><sub>2</sub>| = 1. Media termed diattenuating (or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dichroic" title="Dichroic">dichroic</a></i> in the sense of polarization), in which only the amplitudes of the two polarizations are affected differentially, may be described using a <a href="/wiki/Hermitian_matrix" title="Hermitian matrix">Hermitian</a> matrix (generally multiplied by a common phase factor). In fact, since <i>any</i> matrix may be written as the product of unitary and positive Hermitian matrices, light propagation through any sequence of polarization-dependent optical components can be written as the product of these two basic types of transformations.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birefringence_Stress_Plastic.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Birefringence_Stress_Plastic.JPG/250px-Birefringence_Stress_Plastic.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Birefringence_Stress_Plastic.JPG/375px-Birefringence_Stress_Plastic.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Birefringence_Stress_Plastic.JPG/500px-Birefringence_Stress_Plastic.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birefringence_Stress_Plastic.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Color pattern of a plastic box showing <a href="/wiki/Birefringence#Stress_induced_birefringence" title="Birefringence">stress-induced birefringence</a> when placed in between two crossed <a href="/wiki/Polarizer" title="Polarizer">polarizers</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In birefringent media there is no attenuation, but two modes accrue a differential phase delay. Well known manifestations of linear birefringence (that is, in which the basis polarizations are orthogonal linear polarizations) appear in optical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_plate" title="Wave plate">wave plates</a>/retarders and many crystals. If linearly polarized light passes through a birefringent material, its state of polarization will generally change, <i>unless</i> its polarization direction is identical to one of those basis polarizations. Since the phase shift, and thus the change in polarization state, is usually wavelength-dependent, such objects viewed under white light in between two polarizers may give rise to colorful effects, as seen in the accompanying photograph.\n</p><p>Circular birefringence is also termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_activity" title="Optical activity">optical activity</a>, especially in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiral" title="Chiral">chiral</a> fluids, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faraday_rotation" title="Faraday rotation">Faraday rotation</a>, when due to the presence of a magnetic field along the direction of propagation. When linearly polarized light is passed through such an object, it will exit still linearly polarized, but with the axis of polarization rotated. A combination of linear and circular birefringence will have as basis polarizations two orthogonal elliptical polarizations; however, the term "elliptical birefringence" is rarely used.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birefringence.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="207" data-file-width="225" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Birefringence.svg/240px-Birefringence.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Birefringence.svg/360px-Birefringence.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Birefringence.svg/480px-Birefringence.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birefringence.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paths taken by vectors in the Poincar&#233; sphere under birefringence. The propagation modes (rotation axes) are shown with red, blue, and yellow lines, the initial vectors by thick black lines, and the paths they take by colored ellipses (which represent circles in three dimensions).</div></div></div>\n<p>One can visualize the case of linear birefringence (with two orthogonal linear propagation modes) with an incoming wave linearly polarized at a 45&#176; angle to those modes. As a differential phase starts to accrue, the polarization becomes elliptical, eventually changing to purely circular polarization (90&#176; phase difference), then to elliptical and eventually linear polarization (180&#176; phase) perpendicular to the original polarization, then through circular again (270&#176; phase), then elliptical with the original azimuth angle, and finally back to the original linearly polarized state (360&#176; phase) where the cycle begins anew. In general the situation is more complicated and can be characterized as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coordinate_rotation" title="Coordinate rotation">rotation</a> in the Poincar&#233; sphere about the axis defined by the propagation modes. Examples for linear (blue), circular (red), and elliptical (yellow) <a href="/wiki/Birefringence" title="Birefringence">birefringence</a> are shown in the figure on the left. The total intensity and degree of polarization are unaffected. If the path length in the birefringent medium is sufficient, the two polarization components of a collimated beam (or <a href="/wiki/Ray_(optics)" title="Ray (optics)">ray</a>) can exit the material with a positional offset, even though their final propagation directions will be the same (assuming the entrance face and exit face are parallel). This is commonly viewed using <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a> <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a>, which present the viewer with two slightly offset images, in opposite polarizations, of an object behind the crystal. It was this effect that provided the first discovery of polarization, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erasmus_Bartholinus" title="Erasmus Bartholinus">Erasmus Bartholinus</a> in 1669.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dichroism">Dichroism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Dichroism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Media in which transmission of one polarization mode is preferentially reduced are called <i><a href="/wiki/Dichroism" title="Dichroism">dichroic</a></i> or <i>diattenuating</i>. Like birefringence, diattenuation can be with respect to linear polarization modes (in a crystal) or circular polarization modes (usually in a liquid).\n</p><p>Devices that block nearly all of the radiation in one mode are known as <i>polarizing filters</i> or simply "<a href="/wiki/Polarizer" title="Polarizer">polarizers</a>". This corresponds to <i>g</i><sub>2</sub>=0 in the above representation of the Jones matrix. The output of an ideal polarizer is a specific polarization state (usually linear polarization) with an amplitude equal to the input wave\'s original amplitude in that polarization mode. Power in the other polarization mode is eliminated. Thus if unpolarized light is passed through an ideal polarizer (where <i>g</i><sub>1</sub>=1 and <i>g</i><sub>2</sub>=0) exactly half of its initial power is retained. Practical polarizers, especially inexpensive sheet polarizers, have additional loss so that\n<i>g</i><sub>1</sub> &lt; 1. However, in many instances the more relevant figure of merit is the polarizer\'s <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_polarization" title="Degree of polarization">degree of polarization</a> or <a href="/wiki/Extinction_ratio" title="Extinction ratio">extinction ratio</a>, which involve a comparison of <i>g</i><sub>1</sub> to <i>g</i><sub>2</sub>. Since Jones vectors refer to waves\' amplitudes (rather than <a href="/wiki/Irradiance" title="Irradiance">intensity</a>), when illuminated by unpolarized light the remaining power in the unwanted polarization will be (<i>g</i><sub>2</sub>/<i>g</i><sub>1</sub>)<sup>2</sup> of the power in the intended polarization.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specular_reflection">Specular reflection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Specular reflection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In addition to birefringence and dichroism in extended media, polarization effects describable using Jones matrices can also occur at (reflective) interface between two materials of different <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a>. These effects are treated by the <a href="/wiki/Fresnel_equations" title="Fresnel equations">Fresnel equations</a>. Part of the wave is transmitted and part is reflected; for a given material those proportions (and also the phase of reflection) are dependent on the <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_incidence_(optics)" title="Angle of incidence (optics)">angle of incidence</a> and are different for the <i>s</i> and <i>p</i> polarizations. Therefore, the polarization state of reflected light (even if initially unpolarized) is generally changed.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brewster-polarizer.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="719" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Brewster-polarizer.svg/350px-Brewster-polarizer.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Brewster-polarizer.svg/525px-Brewster-polarizer.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Brewster-polarizer.svg/700px-Brewster-polarizer.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brewster-polarizer.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stack of plates at Brewster\'s angle to a beam reflects off a fraction of the <i>s</i>-polarized light at each surface, leaving (after many such plates) a mainly <i>p</i>-polarized beam.</div></div></div>\n<p>Any light striking a surface at a special angle of incidence known as <a href="/wiki/Brewster%27s_angle" title="Brewster\'s angle">Brewster\'s angle</a>, where the reflection coefficient for <i>p</i> polarization is zero, will be reflected with only the <i>s</i>-polarization remaining. This principle is employed in the so-called "pile of plates polarizer" (see figure) in which part of the <i>s</i> polarization is removed by reflection at each Brewster angle surface, leaving only the <i>p</i> polarization after transmission through many such surfaces. The generally smaller reflection coefficient of the <i>p</i> polarization is also the basis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polarized_sunglasses" title="Polarized sunglasses">polarized sunglasses</a>; by blocking the <i>s</i> (horizontal) polarization, most of the glare due to reflection from a wet street, for instance, is removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hecht2002_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hecht2002-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>348&#8211;350</span></sup>\n</p><p>In the important special case of reflection at normal incidence (not involving anisotropic materials) there is no particular <i>s</i> or <i>p</i> polarization. Both the <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> polarization components are reflected identically, and therefore the polarization of the reflected wave is identical to that of the incident wave. However, in the case of circular (or elliptical) polarization, the handedness of the polarization state is thereby reversed, since by <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization#Left.2FRight" title="Circular polarization">convention</a> this is specified relative to the direction of propagation. The circular rotation of the electric field around the <i>x-y</i> axes called "right-handed" for a wave in the <i>+z</i> direction is "left-handed" for a wave in the <i>-z</i> direction. But in the general case of reflection at a nonzero angle of incidence, no such generalization can be made. For instance, right-circularly polarized light reflected from a dielectric surface at a grazing angle, will still be right-handed (but elliptically) polarized. Linear polarized light reflected from a metal at non-normal incidence will generally become elliptically polarized. These cases are handled using Jones vectors acted upon by the different Fresnel coefficients for the <i>s</i> and <i>p</i> polarization components.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement_techniques_involving_polarization">Measurement techniques involving polarization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Measurement techniques involving polarization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Some optical measurement techniques are based on polarization. In many other optical techniques polarization is crucial or at least must be taken into account and controlled; such examples are too numerous to mention.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement_of_stress">Measurement of stress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Measurement of stress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarized_Stress_Glasses.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1203" data-file-width="2430" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Polarized_Stress_Glasses.jpg/220px-Polarized_Stress_Glasses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Polarized_Stress_Glasses.jpg/330px-Polarized_Stress_Glasses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Polarized_Stress_Glasses.jpg/440px-Polarized_Stress_Glasses.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polarized_Stress_Glasses.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stress in plastic glasses</div></div></div><p>In <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, the phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Birefringence#Stress_induced_birefringence" title="Birefringence">stress induced birefringence</a> allows for stresses in transparent materials to be readily observed. As noted above and seen in the accompanying photograph, the chromaticity of birefringence typically creates colored patterns when viewed in between two polarizers. As external forces are applied, internal stress induced in the material is thereby observed. Additionally, birefringence is frequently observed due to stresses "frozen in" at the time of manufacture. This is famously observed in <a href="/wiki/Cellophane" title="Cellophane">cellophane</a> tape whose birefringence is due to the stretching of the material during the manufacturing process.\n</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ellipsometry">Ellipsometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ellipsometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ellipsometry" title="Ellipsometry">Ellipsometry</a></div>\n<p>Ellipsometry is a powerful technique for the measurement of the optical properties of a uniform surface. It involves measuring the polarization state of light following specular reflection from such a surface. This is typically done as a function of incidence angle or wavelength (or both). Since ellipsometry relies on reflection, it is not required for the sample to be transparent to light or for its back side to be accessible.\n</p><p>Ellipsometry can be used to model the (complex) refractive index of a surface of a bulk material. It is also very useful in determining parameters of one or more <a href="/wiki/Thin_film" title="Thin film">thin film</a> layers deposited on a substrate. Due to their <a href="/wiki/Thin-film_interference" title="Thin-film interference">reflection properties</a>, not only are the predicted magnitude of the <i>p</i> and <i>s</i> polarization components, but their relative phase shifts upon reflection, compared to measurements using an ellipsometer. A normal ellipsometer does not measure the actual reflection coefficient (which requires careful photometric calibration of the illuminating beam) but the ratio of the <i>p</i> and <i>s</i> reflections, as well as change of polarization ellipticity (hence the name) induced upon reflection by the surface being studied. In addition to use in science and research, ellipsometers are used <a href="/wiki/Ellipsometry#In_situ_ellipsometry" title="Ellipsometry">in situ</a> to control production processes for instance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoldsteinGoldstein2011_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoldsteinGoldstein2011-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>585ff</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mansuripur2009_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mansuripur2009-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>632</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LvMS-Lvm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2328" data-file-width="1770" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/LvMS-Lvm.jpg/220px-LvMS-Lvm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/LvMS-Lvm.jpg/330px-LvMS-Lvm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/LvMS-Lvm.jpg/440px-LvMS-Lvm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LvMS-Lvm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photomicrograph of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic" title="Volcanic">volcanic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sand_grain" title="Sand grain">sand grain</a>; upper picture is plane-polarized light, bottom picture is cross-polarized light, scale box at left-center is 0.25 millimeter.</div></div></div>\n<p>The property of (linear) birefringence is widespread in crystalline <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>, and indeed was pivotal in the initial discovery of polarization. In <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">mineralogy</a>, this property is frequently exploited using polarization <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscopes</a>, for the purpose of identifying minerals. See <a href="/wiki/Optical_mineralogy" title="Optical mineralogy">optical mineralogy</a> for more details.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wayne2013_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wayne2013-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>163&#8211;164</span></sup>\n</p><p>Sound waves in solid materials exhibit polarization. Differential propagation of the three polarizations through the earth is a crucial in the field of <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a>. Horizontally and vertically polarized seismic waves (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shear_waves" title="Shear waves">shear waves</a>)are termed SH and SV, while waves with longitudinal polarization (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compressional_wave" title="Compressional wave">compressional waves</a>) are termed P-waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shearer2009_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shearer2009-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>48&#8211;50</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SteinWysession2009_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-SteinWysession2009-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>56&#8211;57</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>We have seen (above) that the birefringence of a type of crystal is useful in identifying it, and thus detection of linear birefringence is especially useful in <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">mineralogy</a>. Linearly polarized light generally has its polarization state altered upon transmission through such a crystal, making it stand out when viewed in between two crossed polarizers, as seen in the photograph, above. Likewise, in chemistry, rotation of polarization axes in a liquid solution can be a useful measurement. In a liquid, linear birefringence is impossible, however there may be circular birefringence when a chiral molecule is in solution. When the right and left handed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enantiomers" title="Enantiomers">enantiomers</a> of such a molecule are present in equal numbers (a so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racemic" title="Racemic">racemic</a> mixture) then their effects cancel out. However, when there is only one (or a preponderance of one), as is more often the case for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecules" title="Organic molecules">organic molecules</a>, a net circular birefringence (or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_activity" title="Optical activity">optical activity</a></i>) is observed, revealing the magnitude of that imbalance (or the concentration of the molecule itself, when it can be assumed that only one enantiomer is present). This is measured using a <a href="/wiki/Polarimeter" title="Polarimeter">polarimeter</a> in which polarized light is passed through a tube of the liquid, at the end of which is another polarizer which is rotated in order to null the transmission of light through it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hecht2002_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hecht2002-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>360&#8211;365</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VollhardtSchore2003_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-VollhardtSchore2003-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polarization_in_astronomy" title="Polarization in astronomy">Polarization in astronomy</a></div>\n<p>In many areas of <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, the study of polarized electromagnetic radiation from <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> is of great importance. Although not usually a factor in the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal radiation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, polarization is also present in radiation from coherent <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Astronomical_source&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Astronomical source (page does not exist)">astronomical sources</a> (e.g. hydroxyl or methanol <a href="/wiki/Maser" title="Maser">masers</a>), and incoherent sources such as the large radio lobes in active galaxies, and pulsar radio radiation (which may, it is speculated, sometimes be coherent), and is also imposed upon starlight by scattering from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_dust" title="Interstellar dust">interstellar dust</a>. Apart from providing information on sources of radiation and scattering, polarization also probes the interstellar magnetic field via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faraday_rotation" title="Faraday rotation">Faraday rotation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>119,124</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KarttunenKr&#246;ger2007_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-KarttunenKr&#246;ger2007-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>336&#8211;337</span></sup> The polarization of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background</a> is being used to study the physics of the very early universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boyle_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-boyle-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tegmark_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-tegmark-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Synchrotron_radiation" title="Synchrotron radiation">Synchrotron radiation</a> is inherently polarised. It has been suggested that astronomical sources caused the <a href="/wiki/Chirality" title="Chirality">chirality</a> of biological molecules on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications_and_examples">Applications and examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Applications and examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarized_sunglasses">Polarized sunglasses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Polarized sunglasses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mudflats-polariser.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="6016" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Mudflats-polariser.jpg/400px-Mudflats-polariser.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Mudflats-polariser.jpg/600px-Mudflats-polariser.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Mudflats-polariser.jpg/800px-Mudflats-polariser.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mudflats-polariser.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Effect of a polarizer on reflection from mud flats. In the picture on the left, the horizontally oriented polarizer preferentially transmits those reflections; rotating the polarizer by 90&#176; (right) as one would view using polarized sunglasses blocks almost all <a href="/wiki/Specular_reflection" title="Specular reflection">specularly reflected</a> sunlight.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Test_for_polarized_and_non-polarized_sunglasses_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="513" data-file-width="683" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Test_for_polarized_and_non-polarized_sunglasses_2.jpg/200px-Test_for_polarized_and_non-polarized_sunglasses_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Test_for_polarized_and_non-polarized_sunglasses_2.jpg/300px-Test_for_polarized_and_non-polarized_sunglasses_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Test_for_polarized_and_non-polarized_sunglasses_2.jpg/400px-Test_for_polarized_and_non-polarized_sunglasses_2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Test_for_polarized_and_non-polarized_sunglasses_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One can test whether sunglasses are polarized by looking through two pairs, with one perpendicular to the other. If both are polarized, all light will be blocked.</div></div></div>\n<p>Unpolarized light, after being reflected by a specular (shiny) surface, generally obtains a degree of polarization. This phenomenon was observed in 1808 by the mathematician <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne-Louis_Malus" title="&#201;tienne-Louis Malus">&#201;tienne-Louis Malus</a>, after whom <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malus%27s_law" title="Malus\'s law">Malus\'s law</a> is named. Polarizing <a href="/wiki/Sunglasses" title="Sunglasses">sunglasses</a> exploit this effect to reduce glare from reflections by horizontal surfaces, notably the road ahead viewed at a grazing angle.\n</p><p>Wearers of polarized sunglasses will occasionally observe inadvertent polarization effects such as color-dependent birefringent effects, for example in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toughened_glass" title="Toughened glass">toughened glass</a> (e.g., car windows) or items made from transparent <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a>, in conjunction with natural polarization by reflection or scattering. The polarized light from LCD monitors (see below) is very conspicuous when these are worn.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sky_polarization_and_photography">Sky polarization and photography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Sky polarization and photography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polarizing_filter_(Photography)" title="Polarizing filter (Photography)">Polarizing filter (Photography)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CircularPolarizer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="946" data-file-width="2484" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CircularPolarizer.jpg/400px-CircularPolarizer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CircularPolarizer.jpg/600px-CircularPolarizer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CircularPolarizer.jpg/800px-CircularPolarizer.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CircularPolarizer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The effects of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polarizing_filter_(Photography)" title="Polarizing filter (Photography)">polarizing filter</a> (right image) on the sky in a photograph.</div></div></div>\n<p>Polarization is observed in the light of the <a href="/wiki/Sky#During_the_day" title="Sky">sky</a>, as this is due to sunlight scattered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerosols" title="Aerosols">aerosols</a> as it passes through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth\'s atmosphere">earth\'s atmosphere</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">scattered</a> light produces the brightness and color in clear skies. This partial polarization of scattered light can be used to darken the sky in photographs, increasing the contrast. This effect is most strongly observed at points on the sky making a 90&#176; angle to the sun. Polarizing filters use these effects to optimize the results of photographing scenes in which reflection or scattering by the sky is involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hecht2002_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hecht2002-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>346&#8211;347</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bekefi_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bekefi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>495&#8211;499</span></sup>\n</p><p>Sky polarization has been used for orientation in navigation. The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pfund_sky_compass&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pfund sky compass (page does not exist)">Pfund sky compass</a> was used in the 1950s when navigating near the poles of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth\'s magnetic field">Earth\'s magnetic field</a> when neither the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a> nor <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> were visible (e.g., under daytime <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">cloud</a> or <a href="/wiki/Twilight" title="Twilight">twilight</a>). It has been suggested, controversially, that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Vikings</a> exploited a similar device (the "<a href="/wiki/Sunstone_(medieval)" title="Sunstone (medieval)">sunstone</a>") in their extensive expeditions across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic" title="North Atlantic">North Atlantic</a> in the 9th&#8211;11th centuries, before the arrival of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_compass" title="Magnetic compass">magnetic compass</a> from Asia to Europe in the 12th century. Related to the sky compass is the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_clock" title="Polar clock">polar clock</a>", invented by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wheatstone" title="Charles Wheatstone">Charles Wheatstone</a> in the late 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pye2001_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pye2001-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>67&#8211;69</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Display_technologies">Display technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Display technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The principle of <a href="/wiki/Liquid-crystal_display" title="Liquid-crystal display">liquid-crystal display</a> (LCD) technology relies on the rotation of the axis of linear polarization by the liquid crystal array. Light from the <a href="/wiki/Backlight" title="Backlight">backlight</a> (or the back reflective layer, in devices not including or requiring a backlight) first passes through a linear polarizing sheet. That polarized light passes through the actual liquid crystal layer which may be organized in pixels (for a TV or computer monitor) or in another format such as a <a href="/wiki/Seven-segment_display" title="Seven-segment display">seven-segment display</a> or one with custom symbols for a particular product. The liquid crystal layer is produced with a consistent right (or left) handed chirality, essentially consisting of tiny <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helices" title="Helices">helices</a>. This causes circular birefringence, and is engineered so that there is a 90 degree rotation of the linear polarization state. However, when a voltage is applied across a cell, the molecules straighten out, lessening or totally losing the circular birefringence. On the viewing side of the display is another linear polarizing sheet, usually oriented at 90 degrees from the one behind the active layer. Therefore, when the circular birefringence is removed by the application of a sufficient voltage, the polarization of the transmitted light remains at right angles to the front polarizer, and the pixel appears dark. With no voltage, however, the 90 degree rotation of the polarization causes it to exactly match the axis of the front polarizer, allowing the light through. Intermediate voltages create intermediate rotation of the polarization axis and the pixel has an intermediate intensity. Displays based on this principle are widespread, and now are used in the vast majority of televisions, computer monitors and video projectors, rendering the previous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" title="Cathode ray tube">CRT</a> technology essentially obsolete. The use of polarization in the operation of LCD displays is immediately apparent to someone wearing polarized sunglasses, often making the display unreadable.\n</p><p>In a totally different sense, polarization encoding has become the leading (but not sole) method for delivering separate images to the left and right eye in <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">stereoscopic</a> displays used for <a href="/wiki/3D_film" title="3D film">3D movies</a>. This involves separate images intended for each eye either projected from two different projectors with orthogonally oriented polarizing filters or, more typically, from a single projector with time multiplexed polarization (a fast alternating polarization device for successive frames). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polarized_3D_glasses" title="Polarized 3D glasses">Polarized 3D glasses</a> with suitable polarizing filters ensure that each eye receives only the intended image. Historically such systems used linear polarization encoding because it was inexpensive and offered good separation. However circular polarization makes separation of the two images insensitive to tilting of the head, and is widely used in 3-D movie exhibition today, such as the system from <a href="/wiki/RealD" title="RealD">RealD</a>. Projecting such images requires screens that maintain the polarization of the projected light when viewed in reflection (such as <a href="/wiki/Silver_screen" title="Silver screen">silver screens</a>); a normal diffuse white projection screen causes depolarization of the projected images, making it unsuitable for this application.\n</p><p>Although now obsolete, CRT computer displays suffered from reflection by the glass envelope, causing glare from room lights and consequently poor contrast. Several anti-reflection solutions were employed to ameliorate this problem. One solution utilized the principle of reflection of circularly polarized light. A circular polarizing filter in front of the screen allows for the transmission of (say) only right circularly polarized room light. Now, right circularly polarized light (depending on the <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization#From_the_point_of_view_of_the_source" title="Circular polarization">convention</a> used) has its electric (and magnetic) field direction rotating clockwise while propagating in the +z direction. Upon reflection, the field still has the same direction of rotation, but now propagation is in the &#8722;z direction making the reflected wave <i>left</i> circularly polarized. With the right circular polarization filter placed in front of the reflecting glass, the unwanted light reflected from the glass will thus be in very polarization state that is <i>blocked</i> by that filter, eliminating the reflection problem. The reversal of circular polarization on reflection and elimination of reflections in this manner can be easily observed by looking in a mirror while wearing 3-D movie glasses which employ left- and right-handed circular polarization in the two lenses. Closing one eye, the other eye will see a reflection in which it cannot see itself; that lens appears black. However the other lens (of the closed eye) will have the correct circular polarization allowing the closed eye to be easily seen by the open one.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_transmission_and_reception">Radio transmission and reception<span id="Antennas"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Radio transmission and reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(radio)#Polarization" title="Antenna (radio)">Antenna (radio) &#167;&#160;Polarization</a></div>\n<p>All <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a> (and microwave) <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(radio)" title="Antenna (radio)">antennas</a> used for transmitting or receiving are intrinsically polarized. They transmit in (or receive signals from) a particular polarization, being totally insensitive to the opposite polarization; in certain cases that polarization is a function of direction. Most antennas are nominally linearly polarized, but elliptical and circular polarization is a possibility. As is the convention in optics, the "polarization" of a radio wave is understood to refer to the polarization of its electric field, with the magnetic field being at a 90 degree rotation with respect to it for a linearly polarized wave.\n</p><p>The vast majority of antennas are linearly polarized. In fact it can be shown from considerations of symmetry that an antenna that lies entirely in a plane which also includes the observer, can <i>only</i> have its polarization in the direction of that plane. This applies to many cases, allowing one to easily infer such an antenna\'s polarization at an intended direction of propagation. So a typical rooftop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yagi-Uda_antenna" title="Yagi-Uda antenna">Yagi</a> or <a href="/wiki/Log-periodic_antenna" title="Log-periodic antenna">log-periodic antenna</a> with horizontal conductors, as viewed from a second station toward the horizon, is necessarily horizontally polarized. But a vertical "<a href="/wiki/Whip_antenna" title="Whip antenna">whip antenna</a>" or AM broadcast tower used as an antenna element (again, for observers horizontally displaced from it) will transmit in the vertical polarization. A <a href="/wiki/Turnstile_antenna" title="Turnstile antenna">turnstile antenna</a> with its four arms in the horizontal plane, likewise transmits horizontally polarized radiation toward the horizon. However, when that same turnstile antenna is used in the "axial mode" (upwards, for the same horizontally-oriented structure) its radiation is circularly polarized. At intermediate elevations it is elliptically polarized.\n</p><p>Polarization is important in radio communications because, for instance, if one attempts to use a horizontally polarized antenna to receive a vertically polarized transmission, the signal strength will be substantially reduced (or under very controlled conditions, reduced to nothing). This principle is used in <a href="/wiki/Satellite_television" title="Satellite television">satellite television</a> in order to double the channel capacity over a fixed frequency band. The same frequency channel can be used for two signals broadcast in opposite polarizations. By adjusting the receiving antenna for one or the other polarization, either signal can be selected without interference from the other.\n</p><p>Especially due to the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Surface_wave#ground_wave" title="Surface wave">ground</a>, there are some differences in propagation (and also in reflections responsible for TV <a href="/wiki/Ghosting_(television)" title="Ghosting (television)">ghosting</a>) between horizontal and vertical polarizations. AM and FM broadcast radio usually use vertical polarization, while television uses horizontal polarization. At low frequencies especially, horizontal polarization is avoided. That is because the phase of a horizontally polarized wave is reversed upon reflection by the ground. A distant station in the horizontal direction will receive both the direct and reflected wave, which thus tend to cancel each other. This problem is avoided with vertical polarization. Polarization is also important in the transmission of <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> pulses and reception of radar reflections by the same or a different antenna. For instance, back scattering of radar pulses by rain drops can be avoided by using circular polarization. Just as specular reflection of circularly polarized light reverses the handedness of the polarization, as discussed above, the same principle applies to scattering by objects much smaller than a wavelength such as rain drops. On the other hand, reflection of that wave by an irregular metal object (such as an airplane) will typically introduce a change in polarization and (partial) reception of the return wave by the same antenna.\n</p><p>The effect of <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">free electrons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a>, in conjunction with the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth\'s magnetic field">earth\'s magnetic field</a>, causes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faraday_rotation#Faraday_rotation_in_the_ionosphere" title="Faraday rotation">Faraday rotation</a>, a sort of circular birefringence. This is the same mechanism which can rotate the axis of linear polarization by electrons in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_space" title="Interstellar space">interstellar space</a> as mentioned <a href="#Astronomy">below</a>. The magnitude of Faraday rotation caused by such a plasma is greatly exaggerated at lower frequencies, so at the higher microwave frequencies used by satellites the effect is minimal. However medium or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short_wave" title="Short wave">short wave</a> transmissions received following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sky_wave" title="Sky wave">refraction by the ionosphere</a> are strongly affected. Since a wave\'s path through the ionosphere and the earth\'s magnetic field vector along such a path are rather unpredictable, a wave transmitted with vertical (or horizontal) polarization will generally have a resulting polarization in an arbitrary orientation at the receiver.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarization_and_vision">Polarization and vision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Polarization and vision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> are capable of perceiving some of the components of the polarization of light, e.g., linear horizontally polarized light. This is generally used for navigational purposes, since the linear polarization of sky light is always perpendicular to the direction of the sun. This ability is very common among the <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, which use this information to orient their <a href="/wiki/Bee_learning_and_communication" title="Bee learning and communication">communicative dances</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pye2001_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pye2001-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>102&#8211;103</span></sup> Polarization sensitivity has also been observed in species of <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mantis_shrimp" title="Mantis shrimp">mantis shrimp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pye2001_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pye2001-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>111&#8211;112</span></sup> In the latter case, one species measures all six orthogonal components of polarization, and is believed to have optimal polarization vision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The rapidly changing, vividly colored skin patterns of cuttlefish, used for communication, also incorporate polarization patterns, and mantis shrimp are known to have polarization selective reflective tissue. Sky polarization was thought to be perceived by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pigeon" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a>, which was assumed to be one of their aids in <a href="/wiki/Homing_pigeon" title="Homing pigeon">homing</a>, but research indicates this is a popular myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The naked <a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">human eye</a> is weakly sensitive to polarization, without the need for intervening filters. Polarized light creates a very faint pattern near the center of the visual field, called <a href="/wiki/Haidinger%27s_brush" title="Haidinger\'s brush">Haidinger\'s brush</a>. This pattern is very difficult to see, but with practice one can learn to detect polarized light with the naked eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pye2001_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pye2001-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>118</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Angular_momentum_using_circular_polarization">Angular momentum using circular polarization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polarization_(waves)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Angular momentum using circular polarization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oceano_Atlantico_1989.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2834" data-file-width="1939" decoding="async" height="292" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Oceano_Atlantico_1989.jpg/200px-Oceano_Atlantico_1989.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Oceano_Atlantico_1989.jpg/300px-Oceano_Atlantico_1989.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Oceano_Atlantico_1989.jpg/400px-Oceano_Atlantico_1989.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oceano_Atlantico_1989.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Circular Polarization through an airplane plastic window 1989</div></div></div>\n<p>It is well known that electromagnetic radiation carries a certain linear <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> in the direction of propagation. In addition, however, light carries a certain <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a> if it is circularly polarized (or partially so). In comparison with lower frequencies such as microwaves, the amount of <a href="/wiki/Spin_angular_momentum_of_light" title="Spin angular momentum of light">angular momentum in light</a>, even of pure circular polarization, compared to the same wave\'s linear momentum (or <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a>) is very small and difficult to even measure. However it was utilized in an experiment to achieve speeds of up to 600 million revolutions per minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 22em; -webkit-column-width: 22em; column-width: 22em;">\n<ul><li><a anisotropy</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glan%E2%80%93Taylor_prism" title="Glan&#8211;Taylor prism">Glan&#8211;Taylor prism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kerr_effect" title="Kerr effect">Kerr effect</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nicol_prism" title="Nicol prism">Nicol prism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Pockels_effect" title="Pockels effect">Pockels effect</a></li>\n<li><a
